Source: Security and Vulnerabilities: Physical, Vectors, and Side-Channel Systems in Semiconductor Architectures
Type: Pasted Text
Security and Vulnerabilities: Physical, Vectors, and Side-Channel Systems in Semiconductor ArchitecturesThe modern semiconductor threat landscape is defined by a convergence of physical access, sophisticated reverse-engineering techniques, and microarchitectural side-channel exploitation.[1, 2, 3] Security can no longer be modeled purely as a logical or software-level boundary.[1, 4] Attackers equipped with consumer-grade analytical tools, high-speed logic analyzers, and custom interposers can routinely intercept external buses, manipulate hardware execution environments, and extract sensitive intellectual property or cryptographic keys.[1, 3, 5] Consequently, establishing a robust security posture requires deep synchronization between silicon-level active protections, secure boot protocols, debug port isolation, hardware-enforced trusted execution environments, and side-channel leakage countermeasures.[1, 6, 7] This report provides an exhaustive, multi-layered technical analysis of these security domains, mapping out their underlying physical mechanisms, mathematical foundations, and modern architectural mitigations.
6.2.8.1. Physical Security: Hardware, Access, and Perimeter HardeningPhysical security forms the outermost boundary of hardware-level defense, aiming to restrict unauthorized physical access, detect physical intrusions, and respond dynamically to breaches before critical parameters are compromised.[8, 9] Under standard security validation frameworks, such as the Federal Information Processing Standards (FIPS) 140-3, physical protection is evaluated through strict levels of evidence and active response.[8]6.2.8.1.1. Tamper-Proof Active Protection and Zeroization ProtocolsFor high-assurance systems, physical protection is achieved by enveloping the sensitive computing perimeter in active, multi-layered detection sensors and silicon-level active meshes.[10, 11, 12]At the printed circuit board (PCB) and enclosure levels, multi-layer laminates utilizing uniaxially-oriented long-chain polymer films, such as polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF), are wrapped around the electronics area.[10, 13] These laminates combine a drill plate designed to detect cutting or drilling with fragile breakwire patterns to detect peeling or disassembly.[13] The drill plate functions as a normally open circuit consisting of conductive flood plains printed on a thin polymer layer.[13] When a drill or cutting tool penetrates the plate, the conductive or non-conductive nature of the penetrator forces the electrodes into contact, creating a high-impedance short-circuit that is easily detected by the system's analog front-end.[13] Conversely, the breakwire pattern operates as a normally closed circuit, using narrow conductive ink traces printed onto the PVDF film.[13] A continuous low electrical current monitors the path's continuity, and any physical manipulation that peels or tears the laminate breaks the breakwire, triggering an immediate open-circuit condition.[13]Complementing these flexible film sensors, capacitive PCB security techniques employ integrated inductor-capacitor (LC) based Capacitance-to-Digital Converters.[14] The use of an LC tank circuit makes the sensor virtually immune to electromagnetic (EM) noise, resolving historical reliability and false-alarm issues associated with capacitive physical sensors.[14] This sensor configuration can log extremely small physical alterations to the PCB, such as drilling or component desoldering, even when the device is completely powered down.[14]At the silicon level, passive top-layer metal shields are vulnerable to physical bypass via micro-soldering, focused ion beam (FIB) trace re-routing, or micro-probing.[11, 12] To counteract these advanced threats, modern security ASICs integrate active mesh shields.[11, 12] An active mesh shield consists of continuous serpentine metal traces routed across the top metallization layers of the silicon die.[11, 12] Instead of carrying static voltage levels, these traces are driven by time-varying, cryptographically generated patterns.[11, 12] These patterns are output by an on-chip cryptographic block cipher or a hardware True Random Number Generator (TRNG).[11, 12] The receiving circuitry dynamically validates the voltage on each trace against the expected cryptographic stream.[11, 12] Any attempt to cut a wire, short adjacent traces, or solder external probes disrupts the synchronized code stream, generating a hardware-level tamper alarm.[11, 12]When any sensor detects a physical breach, the system triggers key zeroization.[1, 15] Zeroization is an irreversible security procedure that erases Critical Security Parameters (CSPs) and Sensitive Security Parameters (SSPs) to prevent forensic data recovery.[15] Unlike standard file deletions, zeroization actively overwrites memory registers with a fixed pattern (such as all zeros, all ones, or cryptographically secure pseudo-random values) or removes power from volatile storage cells.[15] Under FIPS 140-3 Security Levels 3 and 4, the zeroisation circuitry must remain operational at all times, ensuring that plaintext keys are fully destroyed within milliseconds of a detected tamper event.[8, 15]6.2.8.1.2. Secure Boot Activation Protocols: Integrity, Cryptographic Signing, and Key HierarchiesSecure boot establishes a cryptographic root of trust, verifying that only authorized, untampered firmware is allowed to execute on the hardware platform.[16, 17, 18]In unified extensible firmware interface (UEFI) systems, the chain of trust is managed through a structured key database held in non-volatile random-access memory (NVRAM).[17] The architecture is anchored by the Platform Key (PK), which is typically an RSA-2048 or stronger public key that controls ownership of the platform firmware.[17] Below the PK is the Key Enrollment Key (KEK) database, which contains certificates used to authenticate updates to the Allowed Signature Database (db) and the Forbidden Signature Database (dbx).[17] To bridge secure boot compatibility with third-party operating systems and custom kernels, modern platforms utilize a pre-bootloader known as a "shim".[17] The shim is digitally signed by a primary authority (such as Microsoft) to pass baseline UEFI validation, and it subsequently manages Machine Owner Keys (MOKs) stored in the /var/lib/shim-signed/mok/ directory.[17] This allows the platform owner to sign and authorize custom bootloaders, such as GRUB, and specific Linux kernel modules without modifying the primary UEFI NVRAM databases.[17]For embedded systems, such as Silicon Labs' Gecko architectures, secure boot is activated by hardcoded bootloader instructions verifying the application image in flash on every boot cycle.[16] The application must contain an ApplicationProperties_t struct, which defines the signature algorithm, image length, and the physical location of the cryptographic signature.[16] The bootloader computes a SHA-256 digest of the application image up to the start of the signature and verifies it using the Elliptic Curve Digital Signature Algorithm (ECDSA) with the NIST P-256 curve.[16] The public key used for this verification is permanently stored either as an immutable manufacturing token or within the Secure Engine One-Time Programmable (OTP) memory.[16]The Open Compute Project (OCP) secure boot guidelines, alongside NIST SP 800-193 (Platform Firmware Resiliency Guidelines), dictate that the secure boot execution path must be designed such that authentication cannot be bypassed by hardware glitching or modifying mutable code.[18] To maintain confidentiality, secure boot platforms can optionally implement device-unique encryption for firmware images.[18] However, this confidentiality protection does not negate the requirement for digital signatures and integrity protection.[18] Decrypted firmware images must remain strictly restricted to internal, secure RAM during validation and execution, preventing plaintext exposure on external memory buses.[18] Historically, this concept has been structured around secure bootloader processes that compute localized decryption keys, read unbound object files, perform validation, and then re-encrypt and authenticate files within protected memory structures to prevent unauthorized replacement of boot-up firmware in modifiable flash or EEPROM.[4]6.2.8.1.3. Secure JTAG and Debug Isolation VariablesThe joint test action group (JTAG) interface is a necessary tool for hardware debugging and boundary scanning, but if left unprotected, it represents an invasive vector for firmware extraction, memory dumping, and register manipulation.[19, 20, 21] To mitigate this risk, modern system-on-chip (SoC) architectures deploy secure JTAG controllers governed by OTP eFuse configurations.[19, 20]In NXP's i.MX series, the System JTAG Controller (SJC) operates across three distinct security modes.[19, 20] By default, the SJC is set to JTAG Enabled Mode (Mode #3), representing low-security operation.[19, 20] To enforce debug protection, developers must program the JTAG_SMODE eFuse to 0x1, switching the SJC to Secure JTAG Mode.[19] In this mode, the JTAG interface is physically locked until a challenge-response authentication protocol is executed.[19, 20] The SJC reads a device-unique challenge value (such as the SOC UUID programmed during manufacturing) and shifts it out via the Test Data Output (TDO) chain.[19] The external debugger must take this challenge, encrypt or process it using a pre-shared secret key, and shift the resulting 56-bit response back through the Test Data Input (TDI) chain.[19] The internal SJC hardware compares the received response against the secret key stored in the SJC_RESP eFuses, dropping the JTAG isolation firewall only on a precise cryptographic match.[19]Developers must also program the BOOT_CFG_LOCK eFuses to permanently lock and prevent further modification of the JTAG security configurations.[19] If a platform requires absolute security with no future debugging capability, the SJC_DISABLE fuse can be blown, permanently deactivating all JTAG TAP features and boundary scan operations.[19, 20]During high-security boot sequences, software can bypass JTAG authentication by writing to the High Assurance Boot JTAG Debug Enable (HAB_JDE) bit.[19] This allows trusted platform initialization software to open JTAG without a challenge-response process.[19] However, to prevent runtime exploit payloads from leveraging this bit, the boot ROM locks the HAB_JDE bit before passing execution control to the application layer.[19] Developers can permanently disable this software-based opening mechanism by blowing the JTAG HAB Enable Override (JTAG_HEO) eFuse.[19]For highly integrated microcontrollers, such as the NXP MIMXRT1064, implementing secure JTAG requires careful software-level pin-multiplexing configurations.[22] During application code execution, developers must ensure that the JTAG pins are not reassigned to standard general-purpose input/output (GPIO) functions.[22] If the application code incorrectly reconfigures the pins (for example, reassigning GPIO_AD_B0_09, which serves as JTAG_TDI), subsequent secure JTAG connection attempts will fail post-boot.[22] The correct pinmux settings must be explicitly locked in software, as demonstrated in Table 5.Table 5: MIMXRT1064 Pin-Multiplexing Configuration for Secure JTAG RetentionIn High Security - Security Enforced (HS-SE) devices (such as Texas Instruments Keystone 3 platforms), JTAG ports are closed by default, and System Firmware locks the JTAG port of the secure M3 core entirely, preventing any software-based opening.[23] To debug the remaining application cores (such as the Cortex-R5 or Cortex-A), debuggers must access the Secure Access Point (Sec-AP) via the JTAG interface.[23]The host PC uses a specialized command utility (such as dbgauth.exe within Code Composer Studio) to read the SOC UID from the Sec-AP.[23] This UID is integrated into an X.509 debug unlock certificate signed using the customer's active private key.[23] The active key is verified by System Firmware against SHA-256 hashes stored in the eFuses, selecting either the Primary Symmetric Key (SMPK) or Backup Symmetric Key (BMPK) depending on the active key revision fuse.[23] The System Firmware parses the certificate, validating that it contains the correct System Firmware Debug Extension and Software Revision Extension.[23] It further verifies that the target UID in the certificate matches the physical SOC UID (unless wildcard unlock allow_wildcard_unlock is explicitly configured in the board configuration during development), that the certificate revision meets the minimum required threshold (min_cert_rev), and that the requested core debug permissions (coreDbgEn for non-secure debugging and coreDbgSecEn for secure debugging) are authorized.[23] Only after this validation is JTAG access dynamically enabled.[23]
6.2.8.2. Hardware Security: Execution, Isolation, and CryptographySecuring data in use requires hardware-enforced isolation of execution environments, cryptographically isolated memory structures, and rigorous mathematical defense against side-channel analysis.[3, 6, 24]6.2.8.2.1. Trusted Execution Environments and TrustZone ArchitecturesTrusted Execution Environments (TEEs) partition a single physical processor into isolated logical environments: the Normal World (Rich Execution Environment) and the Secure World.[6, 25, 26] While the security goals remain identical across ARM Cortex-A and Cortex-M processors, their hardware implementations differ significantly to accommodate their respective performance and real-time constraints.[26, 27, 28]ARM TrustZone for Cortex-A processors establishes isolation at the system-on-chip (SoC) level.[6, 26] The physical CPU transitions between Secure and Non-secure states through a software monitor running in Exception Level 3 (EL3).[6, 26, 28] To request a secure service, the non-secure operating system must execute a Secure Monitor Call (SMC) instruction, triggering a hardware exception that switches execution context to EL3.[6, 26, 29]The Secure Monitor saves the Non-secure registers, restores the Secure registers, and branches to the Secure TEE core (such as OP-TEE).[6, 29] Peripherals and memory ranges are partitioned at the system bus level using physical controllers, such as the TrustZone Address Space Controller (TZASC) and TrustZone Peripheral Controller (TZPC).[6] Every bus transaction carries a hardware-level Non-Secure (NS) status bit; if the NS bit is asserted and a transaction attempts to access secure memory or a secure peripheral, the hardware blocks the transaction and generates a system bus error.[6, 26]TrustZone for Cortex-M processors is designed for microcontrollers requiring low context-switching overhead and deterministic, real-time interrupt response times.[27, 28] To eliminate the context-saving latency of the software EL3 Secure Monitor, the Cortex-M implementation is entirely memory-mapped, and security transitions are handled automatically in hardware.[27, 28, 30] The security state of the core is determined dynamically by the physical address from which the current instruction is fetched.[27, 28, 30] The address space is partitioned into Secure, Non-secure, and Non-secure Callable (NSC) regions using the internal programmable Secure Attribution Unit (SAU) and the external Implementation Defined Attribution Unit (IDAU).[30]To call a secure function, the Non-secure code must branch directly to an authorized transition gate (veneer) located within the NSC region.[28, 30, 31] This veneer must start with the dedicated Secure Gateway (SG) instruction.[28, 30, 31] When the hardware executes the SG instruction at an NSC address, the core automatically transitions to the Secure state.[28, 30]Register banking is expanded in TrustZone-M; the processor maintains separate physical stack pointers for both security domains.[28, 30, 31] In addition to the standard Main Stack Pointer (MSP) and Process Stack Pointer (PSP) used in ARMv7-M, the ARMv8-M architecture implements:MSP_S (Secure Main Stack Pointer) [28, 30]PSP_S (Secure Process Stack Pointer) [28, 31]MSP_NS (Non-secure Main Stack Pointer) [28, 30]PSP_NS (Non-secure Process Stack Pointer) [28, 30]To prevent stack-overflow attacks from breaching security domains, the hardware integrates dedicated, banked stack limit registers.[28, 31] These registers automatically enforce physical boundary checks on stack growth during execution, raising a usage fault if an operation attempts to exceed the allocated stack limits.[28, 31] Memory protection is further managed by dual-banked Memory Protection Units (MPUs); for example, the STM32L5 supports up to 8 independent MPU regions for the secure domain and 8 regions for the non-secure domain.[28]6.2.8.2.2. Side-Channel Analysis CountermeasuresSide-channel analysis (SCA) exploits physical parameters—such as timing variations, power fluctuations, and electromagnetic (EM) emissions—to extract cryptographic keys or proprietary algorithm configurations.[24, 32, 33] To model power-based side channels (Simple Power Analysis, Differential Power Analysis, and Correlation Power Analysis), security engineers analyze CMOS semiconductor structures.[34, 35] CMOS power consumption is divided into static and dynamic components.[34] Static power consumption arises from physical leakage current (I_{\text{leakage}}) across the transistor gates, modeled as:P_{\text{static}} = I_{\text{leakage}} \cdot V_{DD} [34]Dynamic power consumption occurs strictly when the physical circuits transition between logical states (switching from 0 to 1 or 1 to 0), expressed as:P_{\text{dynamic}} = \alpha \cdot C_L \cdot V_{DD}^2 \cdot f [34]Where \alpha represents the activity factor (fraction of switching transistors), C_L is the load capacitance, V_{DD} is the operating supply voltage, and f is the switching frequency.[34] Because dynamic power is directly proportional to the activity factor (\alpha), the total power consumption of the chip varies in correlation with the data processed.[34, 35] Attackers exploit this data-dependent power variation by measuring thousands of power traces during cryptographic operations (such as AES S-box lookups) and applying Correlation Power Analysis (CPA).[35] By correlating actual power traces with hypothetical power models (such as the Hamming weight of intermediate values), the correct key is statistically isolated at the point of maximum correlation.[35]To mitigate these physical vulnerabilities, semiconductor designs deploy a combination of hardware and software countermeasures [7]:Cryptographic Masking: Mathematically divides intermediate cryptographic values into multiple randomized shares (e.g., splitting a secret byte into arithmetic and boolean masking domains).[7, 36] This ensures that no individual intermediate value processed by the hardware correlates with the secret key, raising the mathematical complexity of the attack to higher-order analyses.[7, 36]Hiding and Noise Injection: Equalizes power signatures across all operations.[7] This is achieved using dual-rail pre-charge logic (where every logic gate draws a constant amount of charge regardless of transition direction) or by dynamically shuffling instruction execution to misalign trace acquisitions.[7, 24, 36] Noise injection utilizes on-chip noise generators or hardware current-domain signature attenuation wrappers (such as STELLAR) to degrade the signal-to-noise ratio of physical side-channels, making statistical correlation practically impossible.[7, 24, 37]Isochronous Execution: Restructures software algorithms to execute in a constant number of clock cycles, independent of input values or secret key bits, neutralizing timing attacks.[7, 24]To evaluate the resilience of these countermeasures, failure analysis labs perform Test Vector Leakage Assessment (TVLA).[36] TVLA uses Welch's t-test to determine if two sets of power traces (one with fixed inputs, one with random inputs) are statistically distinguishable.[36] If the calculated t-value remains within established limits (typically \lvert t \rvert < 4.5), the design is verified as resistant to first-order side-channel leakage.[36]6.2.8.2.3. Secure Memory Structures and Inline EncryptionSecuring data-in-use requires extending the processor's security boundary to encompass external, untrusted physical memory paths.[3, 38, 39] Modern x86 and ARM architectures implement Inline Memory Encryption (IME) directly inside the memory controller to encrypt and decrypt cache lines on the fly.[3, 39, 40]Intel Total Memory Encryption (TME) and Total Memory Encryption - Multi-Key (TME-MK) secure external memory buses using AES-XTS with 128-bit or 256-bit keys.[40] AES-XTS is a block-oriented encryption mode that incorporates the physical address of the data as a cryptographic "tweak".[40] This address-dependent tweaking ensures that identical plaintexts stored at different physical memory locations yield completely distinct ciphertexts, thereby preventing block-relocation and page-aliasing attacks.[40]Under TME-MK, the system allocates page-granular KeyIDs.[40] These KeyIDs are assigned via the upper physical address bits within Extended Page Tables (EPT) or standard IA-32 page tables.[40] The memory controller reads these bits and maps them to an internal, hardware-secured key table, applying the corresponding cryptographic key to the targeted memory page.[40]AMD architectures implement memory encryption through Transparent Secure Memory Encryption (TSME) and Secure Encrypted Virtualization (SEV).[41, 42] SEV assigns a unique cryptographic key per virtual machine (VM), isolating guest VMs from each other and from the hypervisor.[42] This technology has evolved across generations to include:SEV-ES (Encrypted State): Encrypts all CPU register contents when a VM stops running or exits to the hypervisor, preventing register-based information leakage.[42]SEV-SNP (Secure Nested Paging): Introduces physical memory integrity protection to prevent hypervisor-based remapping and data replay attacks.[42, 43]SEV-TIO (Trusted I/O): Extends the TEE boundaries to PCIe devices (such as accelerators and NICs) via the TEE Device Interface Security Protocol (TDISP), enabling secure, authenticated, and isolated direct memory access (DMA) transactions.[42]Despite these protections, baseline AES-XTS lacks cryptographic integrity and replay protection.[44, 45, 46] If an attacker intercepts the physical memory bus, they can perform replay attacks by writing historically valid ciphertext back to the memory bus, or alter data blocks to cause predictable application faults.[44, 45] To defend against these vectors, high-assurance systems combine AES-CTR mode encryption with Message Authentication Codes (MACs) and hardware-traversed Merkle Trees.[44] The Merkle Tree maintains cryptographic hashes of all memory blocks, with the root hash stored in secure, on-chip SRAM.[44] On every memory read, the controller recursively verifies the hash path from the physical memory line up to the root, detecting and blocking any unauthorized modification or data rollback.[44]
Comparative Analysis of Secure Execution and Isolation FrameworksTable 6 provides a systematic comparison of secure execution, memory protection, and hardware-isolation mechanisms discussed in this section.Table 6: Comparative Matrix of Hardware-Level Execution and Isolation Technologies
6.2.8.3. Attack Vectors: Vulnerabilities, Threats, and Physical CompromisePhysical and logical access enables adversaries to execute sophisticated, non-destructive, and destructive exploitation pipelines targeting semiconductor hardware.[2, 47, 48]6.2.8.3.1. Reverse Engineering: Deprocessing, Delayering, and Code ExtractionSemiconductor reverse engineering is a multi-stage failure analysis pipeline used to reconstruct physical layouts, extract netlists, or dump embedded firmware.[49, 50]The process begins with physical decapsulation to strip away the protective plastic or ceramic packaging and expose the silicon die.[49, 50] This is performed using chemical wet etching (fuming nitric or sulfuric acid), mechanical grinding, or laser ablation.[49, 50] Once the die is exposed, delayering is performed to systematically remove individual fabrication layers (passivation, metal interconnects, oxide insulation, and transistor gates).[49, 50, 51] This is achieved through a combination of chemical wet etching, reactive ion (dry) etching, and Chemical-Mechanical Polishing (CMP).[49, 50, 51]On advanced sub-10nm FinFET or Gate-All-Around (GAA) architectures, traditional mechanical polishing causes severe layer warpage and micro-cracking.[49, 52] Modern failure analysis labs resolve this by deploying automated plasma Focused Ion Beam (FIB) systems equipped with Gas-Assisted Etch (GAE) chemistry.[52] Integrated with Python scripting APIs, the plasma FIB-SEM perform high-precision, large-area planar delayering in-situ, removing metal layers with nanometer-scale accuracy.[52]At each exposed layer, high-resolution Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) captures secondary electron images.[49, 51] These image tiles are computationally processed through tile stitching to form full-layer panoramas, and pixel dimensions are calibrated against reference structures to maintain dimensional accuracy.[49] Layout reconstruction software subsequently identifies transistors, capacitors, and vias, generating a gate-level netlist of the proprietary design.[49, 50]For non-intrusive reverse engineering, researchers utilize Side-Channel Disassemblers (SCDs).[53] Instead of physically delayering the die, an SCD translates passive physical emanations (power consumption or EM signatures) into assembly code, enabling runtime code reconstruction and firmware reverse engineering even on encrypted or tamper-protected devices.[53] The side-channel disassembler pipeline is executed in six distinct steps:Power Trace Collection: High-speed oscilloscopes collect time-varying power consumption traces from a training device running known target instructions.[53]Continuous Wavelet Transform (CWT): The collected time-varying power traces are mathematically mapped into the joint time-frequency domain using CWT to isolate transient frequency signatures.[53]Feature Selection and Normalization: Features are selected and normalized using Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence combined with covariate shift adaptation to isolate instruction-dependent signatures from background noise.[53]Dimensionality Reduction: Principal Component Analysis (PCA) reduces the feature dimensionality to optimize downstream data analysis.[53]Classifier Training: Machine learning (ML) classifiers are trained on the reduced feature space to generate reference templates and trace decision boundaries.[53]Instruction Classification: Power traces collected from the target device under assessment are classified against the generated templates, producing reconstructed assembly code.[53]SCD can be deployed defensively as an in-field Control-Flow Integrity (CFI) enforcement mechanism.[53] The hardware-firmware attestation system monitors runtime power or EM profiles, comparing them against the golden program control flow to flag and halt execution if anomalous instructions (indicative of malware or buffer overflow exploits) are detected.[53]6.2.8.3.2. Fault Injection Attacks: Physics, Metastability, and BypassesFault Injection Attacks (FIAs) are active physical manipulations designed to force hardware sequential elements into erroneous states, disrupting the device's logical flow.[47, 54, 55] The underlying physical mechanisms and spatial-temporal constraints of key fault vectors include:Voltage Glitching: Momentarily drops or spikes the operating voltage supply rail (V_{DD}) for nanosecond intervals.[55, 56, 57] This under-powering alters transistor threshold voltages, slowing down signal propagation through combinational logic paths.[56, 57] Consequently, the signals fail to settle before the next clock edge, causing setup-time violations.[56, 57] The voltage level at the destination D flip-flop input lands precisely between logical '0' and '1', inducing metastability.[54] Once the clock edge transitions, the flip-flop resolves this metastable state to an incorrect logic value, resulting in instruction corruption or skipped branch evaluations.[54, 56, 57]Clock Glitching: Compresses or alters the system clock's duty cycle.[47, 55, 56] By shortening the clock period below the circuit's minimum propagation delay, sequential paths are forced to sample combinational nodes before the signals have stabilized, producing predictable setup-time violations and state corruption.[56, 57]Electromagnetic Fault Injection (EMFI): Uses localized, high-intensity transient magnetic pulses from micro-probes positioned over the silicon die.[47, 54, 56] These magnetic pulses induce localized eddy currents within the silicon substrate and metal interconnects.[47, 56] These induced currents alter the internal node voltages, disrupting register states or corrupting instructions directly within the CPU instruction-decode pipeline.[54, 56]Laser Fault Injection (LFI): Directs focused infrared or visible lasers through the backside of decapsulated silicon.[54, 55] The laser light creates electron-hole pairs via the photoelectric effect (Photoelectrical Laser Stimulation), generating localized photocurrents in reverse-biased PN junctions.[54, 55] This semi-invasive technique provides sub-micron spatial and picosecond temporal precision, allowing attackers to flip single target bits in SRAM cells, registers, or peripheral lock registers.[54, 55]By applying these glitching vectors at precise temporal delays following a trigger point (such as a serial transmission start or reset signal), attackers can achieve specific exploitation objectives.[56] These objectives include bypassing cryptographic signature validation during secure boot, circumventing internal flash readout protections (such as the chip.fail exploit) to dump firmware, re-enabling locked hardware debug ports (such as debug resurrection attacks), or bypassing memory range validations at privilege boundaries.[56]6.2.8.3.3. Physical Tampering: Bus Snooping, Probing, and Access CompromisePhysical tampering leverages direct access to tap external, high-speed interconnect buses on the printed circuit board, such as SPI lines or DDR memory buses.[3, 38, 58]Board-level bus sniffing is executed by soldering micro-probes or inserting specialized interposers between memory modules and the host CPU.[38, 48] Once attached, a logic analyzer or oscilloscope captures the physical signal transitions, allowing the attacker to reconstruct the data being transferred.[38, 58]A prime example is address-bus snooping, such as the MEMBUSTER attack.[48] While CPUs supporting hardware enclaves (such as Intel SGX) encrypt the data payload traversing the memory bus, the physical memory addresses often remain unencrypted because standard DRAM chips lack on-chip decryption engines.[38, 48] An attacker with physical access can snoop the unencrypted address bus and reconstruct the victim enclave's memory access patterns.[48] To isolate sensitive patterns from noisy bus traces, the MEMBUSTER attack utilizes three specific techniques:Cache Squeezing: Artificially limits the enclave's cache allocation by filling competing cache sets, forcing the enclave to generate frequent cache misses.[48] This makes execution-dependent memory accesses visible on the external address bus.[48]Critical Page Whitelisting: Allocates only a minimal set of physical pages to the enclave, ensuring that accesses to non-critical pages are kept in the cache while sensitive operations must continuously pull from external DRAM.[48]Oracle-Based Fuzzy Matching: Generates an oracle mapping secret states to memory access patterns, and applies fuzzy matching on the captured noisy bus trace to identify and extract sensitive data.[48]To counter physical bus probing and interposer insertion, modern SoC designs integrate Time-Domain Reflectometry (TDR) directly inside the high-speed bus I/O interfaces.[38] The TDR transmitter uses the active data transmission stream as a stimulus signal, sending high-frequency pulses down the PCB traces.[38] The receiver measures the reflected signals to extract an electrical impedance profile over the trace distance.[38] Because any added physical probe, solder point, or interposer alters the transmission line's capacitive loading and characteristic impedance, the TDR immediately detects and localizes the physical tamper attempt at runtime.[38] Additionally, the extracted unique impedance profile can be processed as a Physical Unclonable Function (PUF) to authenticate the physical bus path, preventing component substitution attacks.[38]
Comparative Analysis of Hardware Attack VectorsTable 7 compiles the hardware-level attack vectors analyzed in this section, comparing their physical mechanisms, typical targets, and standard defensive countermeasures.Table 7: Structural Matrix of Physical and Logical Attack Vectors
6.2.8.4. Technical Synthesis and ConclusionsSecuring semiconductor architectures against modern physical and logical threats requires a transition from perimeter-focused security models to decentralized, continuous verification.[1, 3, 39] The vulnerabilities analyzed across secure boot, debug ports, memory controllers, and interconnect buses demonstrate that software-only boundaries are insufficient when an adversary has physical access to the device.[1, 48, 56]To achieve high-assurance standards such as FIPS 140-3 and defend against sophisticated attackers, modern SoC designs must integrate the following core hardware hardening strategies:Cryptographically Dynamic Perimeter Defense: Enclosure protection must utilize active, multi-layer laminates capable of detecting drilling, cutting, and peeling through continuous electrical monitoring.[10, 13] At the silicon level, passive top-layer shields must be replaced with active meshes driven by randomized, cryptographically generated rolling patterns synchronized with a hardware TRNG.[11, 12] Any modification of these meshes must immediately trigger hardware-level key zeroization to securely wipe all CSPs.[12, 15]Enforced Memory Integrity and Replay Protection: Secure memory architectures must go beyond baseline AES-XTS encryption, which lacks integrity validation and remains vulnerable to bus replay and relocation exploits.[44, 45, 46] High-assurance designs must couple inline memory encryption engines with hardware-traversed Merkle Trees and version counters.[44] This ensures that any data manipulation or rollback attempt on external buses is intercepted before execution.[3, 44]Strict Cryptographic Debug Isolation: Standard JTAG and debug interfaces must be permanently locked using OTP eFuses during manufacturing.[19, 20] Production-grade debugging must require multi-stage cryptographic challenge-response protocols or signed X.509 debug unlock certificates validated by isolated System Firmware.[19, 23] Additionally, software-based debug hooks must be locked before application execution to prevent exploit payloads from dynamically opening debug interfaces.[19]Active Bus Diagnostics and Reflectometry: Highly vulnerable board-level and package-level interconnects, such as memory and storage buses, must incorporate active physical-layer diagnostics.[38] Integrating on-chip Time-Domain Reflectometry (TDR) allows the system to continuously capture physical impedance profiles using the active data streams.[38] This provides real-time detection and localization of any probe attachment or interposer insertion, neutralizing physical bus tapping before data transmission begins.[38]Side-Channel Aware Co-Design: Protection against passive and active side-channel attacks must be integrated at both the hardware and software layers.[7] Cryptographic cores must implement arithmetic and boolean masking, mask conversions, and dynamic hiding techniques.[7, 36] Furthermore, software libraries must enforce isochronous execution paths to eliminate data-dependent timing variations.[7, 24]By co-designing physical-layer active protections, hardware-enforced trusted environments, inline authenticated memory encryption, and rigorous side-channel countermeasures, semiconductor architects can build resilient hardware systems capable of maintaining confidentiality, integrity, and operational security under advanced physical threats.[3, 6, 7, 44]

Source: Semiconductor Reverse Engineering: The Complete Process Explained - Lumenci

Semiconductor Reverse Engineering: The Complete Process Explained - Lumenci
Announcement: Introducing iLumOS by Lumenci: Expert-Powered AI Platform for Patent Intelligence Read More >

Far from a single inspection step, the process spans physical decapsulation, layer-by-layer delayering, nanoscale imaging, circuit reconstruction, netlist extraction, and firmware analysis. Modern chips operate as complex black boxes. Critical innovations are embedded within silicon layouts, signal-processing architectures, and hardware-software interactions that are completely invisible from the outside. RE is therefore often the only practical method for determining how a device functions internally at the implementation level. In reverse engineering intellectual property contexts, this matters because patent claims are written against implementations, not intentions and the gap between the two can only be closed through direct technical analysis of the device itself. Explore Lumenci's Reverse Engineering Services This guide walks through each component of the semiconductor RE process in detail, explaining the technical methods used, the precision required, and why each stage matters for extracting accurate, defensible technical findings.
Sample Selection and Preparation The RE process begins with sample selection, a step that is frequently underestimated. The selected device must represent the exact commercial implementation under analysis. Variants of the same chip family can differ meaningfully in metal layers, feature enablement, or firmware revision, so selecting the wrong variant can invalidate downstream findings entirely. Once selected, the chip undergoes decapsulation: the removal of the protective IC packaging to expose the silicon die. Depending on chip type and packaging material, this is performed using chemical etching (fuming nitric or sulfuric acid), mechanical grinding, or laser ablation. Each method carries tradeoffs between speed, surface cleanliness, and risk of thermal or chemical damage to the die surface. Without decapsulation, the transistor structures, metal interconnects, and memory cells inside the chip remain entirely inaccessible. This step is the entry point to everything that follows.
Die Inspection and Initial Characterization After decapsulation, non-destructive techniques are used to characterize the die before any material is removed. Infrared (IR) microscopy enables through-silicon inspection, allowing analysts to visualize internal structures such as metal interconnects, memory arrays, and through-silicon vias (TSVs) without physically damaging the die. Scanning Acoustic Microscopy (SAM) is used to assess package integrity and identify delamination or voids. Following these non-destructive passes, the exposed die is examined using optical microscopy to establish a high-level layout map: die size, block segmentation, pad locations, alignment features, and the overall arrangement of functional regions such as memory arrays, logic cores, analog sections, and I/O interfaces. X-ray imaging may also be used at this stage to visualize internal structures without consuming the sample. This preliminary characterization defines the scope of the RE effort and guides decisions about which regions of the die require the deepest analysis.
Delayering Delayering is one of the most technically demanding stages of the entire process. Modern chips are fabricated with multiple stacked layers of metals, dielectrics, and semiconductor structures. To analyze these layers, they must be removed sequentially and with tight precision. Techniques include chemical wet etching, plasma (dry) etching, and chemical-mechanical polishing (CMP), chosen based on material composition and layer type. Critical process parameters such as etch rate, temperature, chemical concentration, and exposure time, must be carefully controlled at each step. Over-etching damages or destroys the layer below; under-etching leaves residual material that obscures features in imaging. In advanced technology nodes such as FinFET and GAAFET architectures, the three-dimensional nature of transistor structures makes delayering significantly more complex. Gate-all-around structures and fin geometries require adapted protocols and greater precision than planar nodes. The goal at every step is clean, artifact-free exposure of each layer so it can be accurately imaged and analyzed.
High-Resolution Imaging Once a layer is exposed, it is captured using high-resolution imaging techniques appropriate to the feature scale and information required: Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM) is the primary tool for nanoscale imaging of exposed metal layers, contacts, and vias. It provides high contrast and resolution sufficient for identifying interconnect routing, cell boundaries, and structural features at advanced nodes. Atomic Force Microscopy (AFM) captures surface topography at sub-nanometer resolution, useful for characterizing features where surface height variation carries structural information. Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM) enables cross-sectional analysis of transistor gates, doping profiles, dielectric layers, and other features that require atomic-scale resolution to interpret correctly. Each layer is imaged systematically to ensure full die coverage. Image quality — contrast, resolution, and freedom from charging artifacts — directly determines the accuracy of reconstruction and circuit extraction downstream. Poor imaging at this stage propagates errors through every subsequent step.
Image Processing and Alignment Raw images from SEM and other instruments cannot be used directly. They must undergo a multi-step processing pipeline before they are analytically useful: Tile stitching: Individual image tiles are computationally assembled into full-layer panoramas that cover the entire die area. Scale calibration: Pixel dimensions are calibrated against known reference measurements to ensure dimensional accuracy throughout the analysis. Distortion correction: Lens distortion, scan nonlinearity, and stage drift are corrected to prevent geometric inaccuracies from accumulating. Layer-to-layer alignment: Successive layer images are registered to each other with sub-pixel precision. Misalignment between layers causes feature displacement and corrupts the 3D model of the chip. Specialized software tools such as ImageJ and Pix2net are used alongside manual verification to maintain spatial consistency. Any cumulative error introduced here — in scale, alignment, or distortion — propagates directly into layout reconstruction and netlist extraction, making this one of the most quality-critical stages in the pipeline.
Layout Reconstruction With processed, aligned images available for each layer, analysts reconstruct the physical layout of the chip by converting its geometric and material structure into a digital model that approximates the original fabrication mask layout. Standard cell calibration is performed first to ensure uniform scaling and reliable identification of repeated logic cell structures. This step corrects for stitching errors, improves layer-to-layer registration, and establishes a consistent reference framework for feature recognition across the die. Analysts then identify and label transistors, vias, contacts, metal routing, and other structures layer by layer, interpreting the patterns to reconstruct how components are arranged and interconnected spatially. The resulting layout reconstruction is the structural foundation that makes circuit extraction possible. This stage requires both pattern recognition expertise and deep knowledge of semiconductor fabrication conventions. Misidentifying a structure, a contact versus a via, or a dummy fill versus an active element, introduces errors that cascade into the netlist and functional interpretation.
Netlist Extraction Netlist extraction converts the reconstructed physical layout into an electrical connectivity model. From the layout, analysts trace how transistors, contacts, vias, and metal interconnects connect to each other across multiple layers, building a complete picture of the circuit's wiring. The extracted connections are then encoded in a machine-readable format, typically SPICE for analog circuits or Verilog/EDIF for digital logic, enabling the circuit to be analyzed, simulated, and compared against other reference designs or specifications. Modern chips may contain billions of connections, making full manual extraction impractical. Automated extraction tools handle the bulk of the work, but manual verification remains essential for complex or ambiguous regions. Errors in netlist extraction can mischaracterize circuit topology, produce incorrect logic functions, or obscure the presence of specific architectural features which makes accuracy at this stage critical for any downstream analysis.
Circuit Analysis and Functional Interpretation With a netlist available, the reconstructed circuit is typically imported into a SPICE-compatible simulator to model its electrical behavior. This enables analysts to move beyond structural description into functional understanding: what does this circuit actually do? Circuit analysis at this stage involves identifying standard logic cells, memory structures, arithmetic units, analog components (comparators, ADCs, DACs), and specialized subsystems such as phase-locked loops (PLLs), power management units, and RF front-end circuits. Each identified block is characterized in terms of its inputs, outputs, operating conditions, and functional role within the larger system. Functional interpretation is the most expertise-intensive stage of the process. It requires analysts to bridge structural evidence, what the circuit looks like, with behavioral knowledge of how semiconductor architectures are designed to operate. This is where circuit blocks get mapped to higher-level functions: a specific arrangement of transistors becomes a particular type of error-correction logic, or a memory read pathway, or a signal conditioning stage.
Firmware and Embedded Software Analysis Many modern chips integrate embedded firmware stored in on-chip memory. When a complete technical understanding requires knowledge of software behavior, RE extends beyond hardware reconstruction to firmware extraction and analysis. Firmware extraction typically involves one or more of the following methods: Memory dumping: Direct readout of on-chip flash or ROM contents via accessible interfaces or custom test setups. JTAG/SWD access: Debug interfaces are used to read internal processor state, firmware, and memory contents where security locks are not active or have been bypassed. Fault injection: Controlled voltage glitching, clock manipulation, or electromagnetic and laser injection can induce execution bypasses that expose firmware contents or disable read-protection mechanisms. Code disassembly: Extracted firmware is disassembled and analyzed to understand the control logic, communication protocols, adaptive algorithms, and hardware abstraction layers implemented in software. Firmware analysis is particularly relevant for chips where patented functionality is implemented partly or wholly in software running on embedded processors. In modern communication and AI inference chips, the hardware-software boundary is often blurred, hardware accelerators execute algorithms whose key innovations may be defined in firmware rather than silicon geometry. A hardware-only RE analysis would miss these implementations entirely.
Technical Findings and Documentation The final stage of semiconductor RE is the consolidation of all findings into structured technical documentation. This includes the complete methodology, imaging data, reconstructed layouts, annotated schematics, extracted netlists, circuit interpretations, and functional analysis results. Documentation standards matter as much as technical quality. Every analytical step must be traceable, from raw image to extracted feature to circuit interpretation to functional conclusion. Logical inferences must be clearly distinguished from directly observed structures. Annotations, cross-sectional views, and layer-by-layer comparisons must be presented with sufficient clarity that a technically qualified reader can follow the reasoning independently. This level of rigor is what separates RE findings that hold up under scrutiny from those that do not. The technical documentation is the tangible output of the entire pipeline; the quality of every preceding stage is ultimately reflected in the clarity and defensibility of what gets written here. Reverse Engineering Intellectual Property Disputes: Technical Case Studies High-profile semiconductor intellectual property reverse engineering disputes have demonstrated repeatedly that abstract technical arguments are insufficient without device-level evidence. The cases below illustrate the specific RE methods that have been central to major disputes and what the technical analysis actually involved at each stage. Apple vs. Samsung: Die-Level Analysis of Touchscreen and Display Circuits Apple v. Samsung Electronics is one of the most extensively documented patent disputes in the semiconductor industry. While public coverage focused on design elements and user interface behavior, the underlying technical validation required detailed die-level analysis of multiple chip categories. RE teams analyzed touchscreen controller ICs, display driver circuits, and memory subsystems from Samsung devices. For touchscreen controllers specifically, the analysis went beyond identifying component presence as analysts traced signal processing pathways through analog front-end circuits and digital processing blocks to understand how user touch events were conditioned, digitized, and interpreted. The question was not whether Samsung used a touchscreen controller, but whether the specific signal processing architecture implemented Apple's patented methods. System-level integration was also examined. Apple's patents described hardware-software interactions for features such as smooth scrolling and pinch-to-zoom. Hardware acceleration features within the chips ie. dedicated signal processing units, were identified through RE and correlated with observed device behavior. This approach allowed experts to determine whether Samsung's implementation replicated the underlying patented mechanism or merely produced a similar end-user result through a different technical path. Visual similarity at the user interface level, the analysis confirmed, is technically meaningless without circuit-level verification. Qualcomm vs. Apple: Multi-Layer Analysis of Baseband and Modem Chips The Qualcomm v. Apple dispute was technically more complex than the Apple-Samsung matter because it centered on wireless communication technologies, namely the baseband processing algorithms, signal modulation and encoding techniques, and power-efficient data transmission mechanisms, rather than visible hardware components. RE teams analyzed modem chips in Apple devices (then using Intel modems) across three parallel workstreams: Physical chip RE: Modem chips were delayered and imaged to reconstruct circuit architectures, with particular focus on DSP (Digital Signal Processing) blocks and RF front-end integration. Identifying these structures required navigating extremely dense, multi-layer interconnect stacks at advanced nodes. Firmware analysis: Modem functionality is substantially controlled by embedded firmware. RE extended to code extraction, disassembly, and protocol behavior analysis to understand how baseband algorithms were implemented in software rather than fixed silicon. Behavioral correlation: Signal throughput measurements and power consumption patterns were measured under controlled conditions and correlated with both the hardware architecture and firmware logic to produce functional evidence linking observed behavior to specific implementation choices. The Qualcomm-Apple matter illustrates a technical challenge that is increasingly common in modern semiconductor RE: many patented features exist simultaneously at the hardware level (in silicon structures), the firmware level (in embedded algorithms), and the system behavior level (in measurable performance characteristics). A complete analysis must integrate all three layers. Hardware RE alone would have identified relevant circuit structures but could not have confirmed whether the patented algorithms were actually executing. Firmware analysis alone could have identified code constructs but could not have confirmed their hardware implementation. Only the combination produced technically complete findings. Conclusion Semiconductor reverse engineering is a multi-stage technical discipline that transforms opaque integrated circuits into fully characterized, analyzable systems. The process — spanning decapsulation, delayering, high-resolution imaging, layout reconstruction, netlist extraction, functional interpretation, and firmware analysis — is not a single activity but a carefully sequenced pipeline in which each stage depends on the precision and quality of the one before it. As semiconductor architectures advance toward smaller nodes, 3D integration, chiplet-based designs, and increasingly software-defined functionality, the technical demands of RE will continue to grow. FinFET and GAAFET geometries complicate delayering. Heterogeneous integration obscures system-level boundaries. Embedded AI inference engines blend hardware and firmware in ways that require both to be analyzed together. What does not change is the fundamental value of the process: the ability to produce objective, verifiable technical evidence about what exists inside a chip and how it functions. Intellectual property reverse engineering depends entirely on this foundation, without implementation-level evidence, IP claims rest on inference rather than fact. In a field where implementation details are intentionally non-public and technical claims are routinely contested, that capability is irreplaceable. For organizations looking to apply semiconductor reverse engineering findings in patent assertion, licensing, or IP strategy, see Semiconductor IP Infringement Analysis Through Reverse Engineering. For a direct consultation, contact Lumenci. FAQs What is semiconductor reverse engineering? Semiconductor reverse engineering is the process of physically deconstructing an integrated circuit, through decapsulation, delayering, imaging, and circuit extraction, to understand its internal architecture and how it functions at the implementation level. How is reverse engineering used in intellectual property analysis? Reverse engineering intellectual property disputes require implementation-level evidence that public documentation cannot provide. RE produces direct technical proof of what exists inside a chip, enabling precise mapping between patent claims and actual device behavior. What is the difference between hardware reverse engineering and firmware reverse engineering? Hardware reverse engineering analyzes the physical silicon ie. transistor structures, metal layers, and circuit topology. Firmware reverse engineering extracts and analyzes the embedded software controlling that hardware. In modern chips, both are often required together because patented functionality can be split across silicon and code. SENSORS FOR TAMPER DETECTION TE Connectivity’s polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) tamper detection sensors offer a complete solution in a space efficient form factor for the detection of physical tampering in small, high security enclosures. The sensor comes in the form of a multi-layer laminate that wraps around the electronics area requiring protection. Once properly adhered and connected, any attempt to physically open or penetrate the enclosure will trigger a detection. Detection of a tamper attempt can result in a customer-specified reaction such as the erasure of critical data, like encryption keys, or disabling the overall system. These tamper detection sensors are designed to allow the user to achieve FIPS 140-2 level 4 physical security compliance while meeting their specific requirements. By passively monitoring the activity of a device, side-channel information can be obtained, e.g. by measuring the variations of the electro-magnetic field over the chip surface or by obtaining power measurements. The information leaked by a device during the computation can be exploited using statistical analysis in so called side-channel attacks. Over the last two decades, a varietey of side-channel attacks have been proposed. On the other hand, many implementation countermeasures have been proposed to thwart side-channel attacks. This quest for novel attacks and countermeasures is still a vivid research area. The main challenge in the field of secure implementations is to find the best trade-off between standard design constraints like speed, area and power consumption and physical security constraints like side-channel countermeasures. The Chair for Security in Information Technology focusses on side-channel analysis and the development of countermeasures for Post-Quantum Cryptography and Neural Networks. Side-channel analysis of neural network implementations is of growing importance as machine learning models are increasingly deployed on resource-constrained and security-critical hardware platforms. SCA exploits physical leakage, such as power consumption, electromagnetic emanations, or timing information, to infer internal computations or model parameters. For neural networks, such leakage can reveal sensitive information including weights, biases, or activation thresholds, effectively exposing proprietary architectures or confidential training data. This poses a significant threat to both intellectual property protection and data privacy, particularly in edge devices, embedded systems, and hardware accelerators where models are executed locally. Consequently, the systematic evaluation of neural hardware against side-channel vulnerabilities is essential to assess and enhance its resilience, inform the design of secure architectures, and develop countermeasures that preserve model confidentiality without compromising computational efficiency. In the advent of Post-Quantum Cryptography, new cryptographic algorithms are proposed and deployed. It is vital for the security of many systems, that possible attacks on these algorithms are well studied. Understanding attacks enables implementations that resist side-channel attacks and allows for the design of appropriate countermeasures. Research topics: Side-channel analysis of Neural Network implementations Side-channel analysis of Post-quantum cryptography Countermeasures against side-channel attacks Course Offered: Secure Implementation of Cryptographic Devices (lecture) SmartCard Lab (project-based practical training) Advanced Cryptographic Implementations (lecture) Selected Publications Probst, Matthias and Tebelmann, Lars and Wettermann, Moritz and Pehl, Michael: Remote side-channel analysis of the loop PUF using a TDC-based voltage sensor. From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia Any attack based on information gained from the implementation of a computer system "Side channels" redirects here. For the yoga concept, see Nadi (yoga) § Side channels. For interactive television services, see Red Button (digital television). An attempt to decode RSA key bits using power analysis. The left pulse represents the CPU power variations during the step of the algorithm without multiplication, the broader right pulse – step with multiplication, allowing an attacker to read bits 0, 1. In computer security, a side-channel attack is a type of security exploit that uses information inadvertently leaked by a system—such as timing, power consumption, or electromagnetic or acoustic emissions—to gain unauthorized access to sensitive information. These attacks differ from those targeting flaws in the design of cryptographic protocols or algorithms (notwithstanding the fact that cryptanalysis may identify vulnerabilities relevant to both types of attacks). Some side-channel attacks require technical knowledge of the internal operation of the system, others such as differential power analysis are effective as black-box attacks. The rise of Web 2.0 applications and software-as-a-service has also significantly raised the possibility of side-channel attacks on the web, even when transmissions between a web browser and server are encrypted (e.g. through HTTPS or WiFi encryption), according to researchers from Microsoft Research and Indiana University. [1] Attempts to break a cryptosystem by deceiving or coercing people with legitimate access are not typically considered side-channel attacks: see social engineering and rubber-hose cryptanalysis. General classes of side-channel attack include: Cache attack – attacks based on attacker's ability to monitor cache accesses made by the victim in a shared physical system as in virtualized environment or a type of cloud service. Timing attack – attacks based on measuring how much time various computations (such as, say, comparing an attacker's given password with the victim's unknown one) take to perform. Power-monitoring attack – attacks that make use of varying power consumption by the hardware during computation. Electromagnetic attack – attacks based on leaked electromagnetic radiation, which can directly provide plaintexts and other information. Such measurements can be used to infer cryptographic keys using techniques equivalent to those in power analysis or can be used in non-cryptographic attacks, e.g. TEMPEST (aka Van Eck phreaking or radiation monitoring) attacks. Acoustic cryptanalysis – attacks that exploit sound produced during a computation (rather like power analysis). Differential fault analysis – in which secrets are discovered by introducing faults in a computation. Data remanence – in which sensitive data are read after supposedly having been deleted. (e.g. cold boot attack) Software-initiated fault attacks – Currently a rare class of side channels, row hammer is an example in which off-limits memory can be changed by accessing adjacent memory too often (causing state retention loss). Whitelist – attacks based on the fact that the whitelisting devices will behave differently when communicating with whitelisted (sending back the responses) and non-whitelisted (not responding to the devices at all) devices. Whitelist-based side channel may be used to track Bluetooth MAC addresses. Optical – in which secrets and sensitive data can be read by visual recording using a high resolution camera, or other devices that have such capabilities (see examples below). In all cases, the underlying principle is that physical effects caused by the operation of a cryptosystem ( on the side) can provide useful extra information about secrets in the system, for example, the cryptographic key, partial state information, full or partial plaintexts and so forth. The term cryptophthora (secret degradation) is sometimes used to express the degradation of secret key material resulting from side-channel leakage. Examples [ edit] A cache side-channel attack works by monitoring security critical operations such as AES T-table entry [2] [3] [4] or modular exponentiation or multiplication or memory accesses. [5] The attacker then is able to recover the secret key depending on the accesses made (or not made) by the victim, deducing the encryption key. Also, unlike some of the other side-channel attacks, this method does not create a fault in the ongoing cryptographic operation and is invisible to the victim. In 2017, two CPU vulnerabilities (dubbed Meltdown and Spectre) were discovered, which can use a cache-based side channel to allow an attacker to leak memory contents of other processes and the operating system itself. Timing attacks monitor data movement into and out of the CPU or memory on the hardware running the cryptosystem or algorithm. Simply by observing variations in how long it takes to perform cryptographic operations, it might be possible to determine the entire secret key. [6] Such attacks involve statistical analysis of timing measurements and have even been demonstrated across networks. [7] A power analysis attack can provide even more detailed information by observing the power consumption of a hardware device such as CPU or cryptographic circuit. These attacks are roughly categorized into simple power analysis (SPA) and differential power analysis (DPA). One example is Collide+Power, which affects nearly all CPUs. [8] [9] [10] Other examples use machine learning approaches. [11] Fluctuations in current also generate radio waves, enabling attacks that analyze measurements of electromagnetic (EM) emanations. These attacks typically involve similar statistical techniques as power-analysis attacks. A deep-learning-based side-channel attack, [12] [13] [14] using the power and EM information across multiple devices has been demonstrated with the potential to break the secret key of a different but identical device in as low as a single trace. Historical analogues to modern side-channel attacks are known. A recently declassified NSA document reveals that as far back as 1943, an engineer with Bell telephone observed decipherable spikes on an oscilloscope associated with the decrypted output of a certain encrypting teletype. [15] According to former MI5 officer Peter Wright, the British Security Service analyzed emissions from French cipher equipment in the 1960s. [16] In the 1980s, Soviet eavesdroppers were suspected of having planted bugs inside IBM Selectric typewriters to monitor the electrical noise generated as the type ball rotated and pitched to strike the paper; the characteristics of those signals could determine which key was pressed. [17] Power consumption of devices causes heating, which is offset by cooling effects. Temperature changes create thermally induced mechanical stress. This stress can create low level acoustic emissions from operating CPUs (about 10 kHz in some cases). Recent research by Shamir et al. has suggested that information about the operation of cryptosystems and algorithms can be obtained in this way as well. This is an acoustic cryptanalysis attack. If the surface of the CPU chip, or in some cases the CPU package, can be observed, infrared images can also provide information about the code being executed on the CPU, known as a thermal-imaging attack. [18] An optical side-channel attack examples include gleaning information from the hard disk activity indicator [19] to reading a small number of photons emitted by transistors as they change state. [20] Allocation-based side channels also exist and refer to the information that leaks from the allocation (as opposed to the use) of a resource such as network bandwidth to clients that are concurrently requesting the contended resource. [21] Countermeasures [ edit] There are two primary categories of measures to counter side-channel attacks: Eliminating or reducing emissions: This involves minimizing the unintended release of signals, such as electromagnetic radiation or timing variations, that attackers could exploit. Transforming the secret data: Typically achieved through randomization, this approach ensures: The cryptographic operation does not leak information that could be correlated with the secret data. A subsequent transformation restores the intended result after the cryptographic operation. Under the first category, displays with special shielding to lessen electromagnetic emissions, reducing susceptibility to TEMPEST attacks, are now commercially available. Power line conditioning and filtering can help deter power-monitoring attacks, although such measures must be used cautiously, since even very small correlations can remain and compromise security. Physical enclosures can reduce the risk of surreptitious installation of microphones (to counter acoustic attacks) and other micro-monitoring devices (against CPU power-draw or thermal-imaging attacks). Another countermeasure (still in the first category) is to jam the emitted channel with noise. For instance, a random delay can be added to deter timing attacks, although adversaries can compensate for these delays by averaging multiple measurements (or, more generally, using more measurements in the analysis). When the amount of noise in the side channel increases, the adversary needs to collect more measurements. Another countermeasure under the first category is to use security analysis software to identify certain classes of side-channel attacks that can be found during the design stages of the underlying hardware itself. Timing attacks and cache attacks are both identifiable through certain commercially available security analysis software platforms, which allow for testing to identify the attack vulnerability itself, as well as the effectiveness of the architectural change to circumvent the vulnerability. The most comprehensive method to employ this countermeasure is to create a Secure Development Lifecycle for hardware, which includes utilizing all available security analysis platforms at their respective stages of the hardware development lifecycle. [22] In the case of timing attacks against targets whose computation times are quantized into discrete clock cycle counts, an effective countermeasure against is to design the software to be isochronous, that is to run in an exactly constant amount of time, independently of secret values. This makes timing attacks impossible. [23] Such countermeasures can be difficult to implement in practice, since even individual instructions can have variable timing on some CPUs. One partial countermeasure against simple power attacks, but not differential power-analysis attacks, is to design the software so that it is "PC-secure" in the "program counter security model". In a PC-secure program, the execution path does not depend on secret values. In other words, all conditional branches depend only on public information. (This is a more restrictive condition than isochronous code, but a less restrictive condition than branch-free code.) Even though multiply operations draw more power than NOP on practically all CPUs, using a constant execution path prevents such operation-dependent power differences (differences in power from choosing one branch over another) from leaking any secret information. [23] On architectures where the instruction execution time is not data-dependent, a PC-secure program is also immune to timing attacks. [24] [25] Another way in which code can be non-isochronous is that modern CPUs have a memory cache: accessing infrequently used information incurs a large timing penalty, revealing some information about the frequency of use of memory blocks. Cryptographic code designed to resist cache attacks attempts to use memory in only a predictable fashion (like accessing only the input, outputs and program data, and doing so according to a fixed pattern). For example, data-dependent table lookups must be avoided because the cache could reveal which part of the lookup table was accessed. Other partial countermeasures attempt to reduce the amount of information leaked from data-dependent power differences. Some operations use power that is correlated to the number of 1 bits in a secret value. Using a constant-weight code (such as using Fredkin gates or dual-rail encoding) can reduce the leakage of information about the Hamming weight of the secret value, although exploitable correlations are likely to remain unless the balancing is perfect. This "balanced design" can be approximated in software by manipulating both the data and its complement together. [23] Several "secure CPUs" have been built as asynchronous CPUs; they have no global timing reference. While these CPUs were intended to make timing and power attacks more difficult, [23] subsequent research found that timing variations in asynchronous circuits are harder to remove. [26] Recently, white-box modeling was utilized to develop a low-overhead generic circuit-level countermeasure [27] against both EM as well as power side-channel attacks. To minimize the effects of the higher-level metal layers in an IC acting as more efficient antennas, [28] the idea is to embed the crypto core with a signature suppression circuit, [29] [30] routed locally within the lower-level metal layers, leading towards both power and EM side-channel attack immunity. Blinding [ edit] Main article: Blinding (cryptography) As a countermeasure for message encryption, masking (also known as blinding) is effective against all side-channel attacks. The principle of masking is to avoid manipulating any sensitive value y {\displaystyle y} directly, but rather manipulate a sharing of it: a set of variables (called "shares") y 1 , ... , y d {\displaystyle y_{1},...,y_{d}} such that y = y 1 ⊕ ... ⊕ y d {\displaystyle y=y_{1}\oplus ...\oplus y_{d}} (where ⊕ {\displaystyle \oplus } is the XOR operation). An attacker must recover all the values of the shares to get any meaningful information. [31] In the case of RSA decryption [6] [7] with secret exponent d {\displaystyle d} and corresponding encryption exponent e {\displaystyle e} and modulus m {\displaystyle m} , the technique applies as follows (for simplicity, the modular reduction by m is omitted in the formulas): before decrypting, that is, before computing the result of y d {\displaystyle y^{d}} for a given ciphertext y {\displaystyle y} , the system picks a random number r {\displaystyle r} and encrypts it with public exponent e {\displaystyle e} to obtain r e {\displaystyle r^{e}} . Then, the decryption is done on y ⋅ r e {\displaystyle y\cdot r^{e}} to obtain ( y ⋅ r e ) d = y d ⋅ r e ⋅ d = y d ⋅ r {\displaystyle {(y\cdot r^{e})}^{d}=y^{d}\cdot r^{e\cdot d}=y^{d}\cdot r} . Since the decrypting system chose r {\displaystyle r} , it can compute its inverse modulo m {\displaystyle m} to cancel out the factor r {\displaystyle r} in the result and obtain y d {\displaystyle y^{d}} , the actual result of the decryption. For attacks that require collecting side-channel information from operations with data controlled by the attacker, blinding is an effective countermeasure, since the actual operation is executed on a randomized version of the data, over which the attacker has no control or even knowledge.
